Domestic Sales  | 17,416,432  | 15,120,783  | 13,570,008  | 15,862,771  | 17,974,365  | 19,607,332   
Export Sales  | 3,519,405  | 3,282,786  | 4,443,131  | 3,652,122  | 3,458,416  | 4,198,403   
  
### Micro, Small, and Medium Enterprises (MSME)

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=16 "Edit
section: Micro, Small, and Medium Enterprises \(MSME\)")]

See also: [Ministry of Micro, Small and Medium
Enterprises](/wiki/Ministry_of_Micro,_Small_and_Medium_Enterprises "Ministry
of Micro, Small and Medium Enterprises")

India began its first few steps during the years 1978-80 when early conditions
for SMEs or entrepreneurship were hostile too. 63 million MSMEs in India which
contribute _35% to the country's GDP_ provides employment to 111.4 million
persons and accounts for more than 40% of India's exports and are hailed as
the 'growth engines' of the economy. China has been creating 16,000-18,000 new
enterprises per day for the last 5 years. When one compares that with India,
it is about 1000-1100 per day.[218]

Micro and small enterprises have the potential to resolve India's unemployment
crisis provided the constraints impeding the growth of the sector are
resolved. According to Annual MSME Report 2021-22, over 90 per cent of India's
6.3 crore MSMEs are in the micro-segment. Within the micro sector, 62 per cent
firms are self-employments which no workers, another 32 per cent have two or
three workers and just 6-7 per cent have four workers or above (up to
19).[219] In 2023, SME IPOs set a record-breaking year with 179 listings.

In Budget 2023, The government has implemented a number of reforms aimed at
boosting MSMEs' growth in India while also improving their international
competitiveness.[220]

### Machinery, Tools and equipment

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=17 "Edit
section: Machinery, Tools and equipment")]

Machinery and equipment market is expected to grow 8% from 2024 to 2029.
India's Industrial Machinery Equipment and Tools market size is expected to be
$210 billion in 2023.The rise in R&D and large number of startups has led to
increase in investment in tools, industrial equipment, robotics, industrial
automation, pharmaceutical machinery, mining & construction equipment.[221]

[Indian Government](/wiki/Indian_Government "Indian Government") has launched
an initiative in promoting electrification of fossil-fuel based equipment
hence reducing carbon footprint and leading to new innovations.[222]

### Mining, resources, and chemicals

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=18 "Edit
section: Mining, resources, and chemicals")]

#### Mining

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=19 "Edit
section: Mining")]

Main articles: [Mining in India](/wiki/Mining_in_India "Mining in India") and
[Iron and steel industry in India](/wiki/Iron_and_steel_industry_in_India
"Iron and steel industry in India")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Mine_II%2C_Neyveli_NLCIL.jpg/250px-
Mine_II%2C_Neyveli_NLCIL.jpg)](/wiki/File:Mine_II,_Neyveli_NLCIL.jpg)an [NLC
India](/wiki/NLC_India_Limited "NLC India Limited") mine

India has, in 2022, a reported 1,319 mines of which reporting mines for
metallic minerals were estimated at 545 and non-metallic minerals at 775.[223]

Mining contributed to 1.75% of GDP and employed directly or indirectly 11
million people in 2021.[224] India's mining industry was the fourth-largest
producer of minerals in the world by volume, and eighth-largest producer by
value in 2009.[225] In 2013, it mined and processed 89 minerals, of which four
were fuel, three were atomic energy minerals, and 80 non-fuel.[226] The public
sector accounted for 68% of mineral production by volume in 2011–12.[227]
India has the world's fourth-largest [natural
resources](/wiki/Natural_resources_of_India "Natural resources of India"),
with the [mining sector](/wiki/Mining_in_India "Mining in India") contributing
11% of the country's industrial GDP and 2.5% of total GDP.

Nearly 50% of India's mining industry, by output value, is concentrated in
eight states: [Odisha](/wiki/Odisha "Odisha"), Rajasthan,
[Chhattisgarh](/wiki/Chhattisgarh "Chhattisgarh"), Andhra Pradesh,
[Telangana](/wiki/Telangana "Telangana"), Jharkhand, [Madhya
Pradesh](/wiki/Madhya_Pradesh "Madhya Pradesh") and
[Karnataka](/wiki/Karnataka "Karnataka"). Another 25% of the output by value
comes from offshore oil and gas resources.[227] India operated about 3,000
mines in 2010, half of which were coal, limestone and iron ore.[228] On
output-value basis, India was one of the five largest producers of mica,
chromite, coal, lignite, iron ore, bauxite, barite, zinc and manganese; while
being one of the ten largest global producers of many other
minerals.[225][227] India was the fourth-largest producer of steel in
2013,[229] and the seventh-largest producer of aluminium.[230]

India's mineral resources are vast.[231] However, its mining industry has
declined – contributing 2.3% of its GDP in 2010 compared to 3% in 2000, and
employed 2.9 million people – a decreasing percentage of its total labour.
India is a net importer of many minerals including coal. India's mining sector
decline is because of complex permit, regulatory and administrative
procedures, inadequate infrastructure, shortage of capital resources, and slow
adoption of environmentally sustainable technologies.[227][232]

#### Cement

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=20 "Edit
section: Cement")]

Indian cement industry is the 2nd largest cement producing country in the
world, next only to China. At present, the Installed Capacity of Cement in
India is 500 MTPA with production of 298 million tonnes per annum. Majority of
the cement plants installed capacity (about 35%) is located in the states of
south India. In PAT scheme, Total Installed Capacity of Cement in India is 325
MTPA which contributes to 65% coverage of total installed capacity in India.

#### Iron and steel

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=21 "Edit
section: Iron and steel")]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Steel_Plant%2C_Bokaro_Steel_City.jpg/250px-
Steel_Plant%2C_Bokaro_Steel_City.jpg)](/wiki/File:Steel_Plant,_Bokaro_Steel_City.jpg)[Bokaro
Steel Plant](/wiki/Bokaro_Steel_Plant "Bokaro Steel Plant") (BSL) alone
contributes 45% of [SAIL](/wiki/Steel_Authority_of_India "Steel Authority of
India")'s profit and it produces highly diversified steel portfolio

India surpassed Japan as the second largest steel producer in January
2019.[233] As per worldsteel, India's crude steel production in 2018 was at
106.5 tonnes (104.8 long tons; 117.4 short tons), 4.9% increase from 101.5
tonnes (99.9 long tons; 111.9 short tons) in 2017, which means that India
overtook Japan as the world's second largest steel production country.

According to data presented by PIB(FY2021-22), there are more than 900 steel
plants in India that produce crude steel. These are owned by PSUs, large-scale
companies as well as small and medium enterprises (SMEs). In the year 2021–22,
the total capacity of these plants stood at 154.06 million tonnes.[234]

The total market value of the Indian steel sector stood at US$57.8 billion in
2011 and is predicted to touch US$95.3 billion by 2016.Growth of crude steel
production in India has not kept pace with the growth in capacity of
production, according to the report. As per this report, steel sector
contributes 2 per cent to India's GDP and employs half a million people
directly and 2 million people indirectly. The Indian steel sector has been
vibrant, growing at a compounded rate of 6% year-on-year.[235] Multiple
integrated steel plants both state-owned and private are using automated AI-
based systems for production.[236]

#### Petroleum

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=22 "Edit
section: Petroleum")]

Main article: [Oil and gas industry in
India](/wiki/Oil_and_gas_industry_in_India "Oil and gas industry in India")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/MRPL_Refinery.jpg/250px-
MRPL_Refinery.jpg)](/wiki/File:MRPL_Refinery.jpg)[MRPL
Refinery](/wiki/Mangalore_Refinery_and_Petrochemicals_Limited "Mangalore
Refinery and Petrochemicals Limited") in [Mangalore](/wiki/Mangalore
"Mangalore")

Petroleum products and chemicals are a major contributor to India's industrial
GDP, and together they contribute over 34% of its export earnings. India hosts
[many oil refinery](/wiki/List_of_oil_refineries_in_India "List of oil
refineries in India") and petrochemical operations developed with help of
[Soviet](/wiki/Soviet_Union "Soviet Union") technology such as [Barauni
Refinery](/wiki/Barauni_Refinery "Barauni Refinery") and [Gujarat
Refinery](/wiki/Gujarat_Refinery "Gujarat Refinery"), it also includes the
world's largest refinery complex in [Jamnagar](/wiki/Jamnagar "Jamnagar") that
processes 1.24 million barrels of crude per day.[237] By volume, the Indian
chemical industry was the third-largest producer in Asia, and contributed 5%
of the country's GDP. India is one of the five-largest producers of
agrochemicals, polymers and plastics, dyes and various organic and inorganic
chemicals.[238] Despite being a large producer and exporter, India is a net
importer of chemicals due to domestic demands.[239] India's [chemical
industry](/wiki/Chemical_industry_in_India "Chemical industry in India") is
extremely diversified and estimated at $178 billion.[240]

#### Chemicals and fertilizer

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=23 "Edit
section: Chemicals and fertilizer")]

The chemical industry contributed $163 billion to the economy in FY18 and is
expected to reach $300–400 billion by 2025.[241][242] The industry employed
17.33 million people (4% of the workforce) in 2016.[243]

At present, 57 large fertilizer units are manufacturing a wide number of
nitrogen fertilizers. These include 29 urea-producing units and 9 ammonia
sulfate-producing units as a by-product. Besides, there are 64 small-scale
producing units of single super phosphate.[244]

According to the latest data released by the WTO, India has emerged as the
second largest exporter of agrochemicals in the world. The rank was sixth, 10
years ago. The Indian agrochemical industry fetches valuable trade surplus
every year. The trade surplus sharply increased from Rs. 8,030 crores in
2017–18 to Rs. 28,908 crores in the last fiscal. India's agrochemicals export
has doubled in the last 6 years from $2.6 bn in 2017–18 to $5.4 bn in the last
financial year according to the data recently released by Ministry of
Commerce. It has grown at an impressive CAGR of 13% which is among the highest
in the manufacturing sector.[245]

Millions of farmers in over 130 countries trust Indian agrochemicals for their
high quality and affordable prices, said an industry observer. With the global
agrochemicals market estimated at $78 billion, predominantly comprising post-
patent products, India is rapidly becoming a preferred global hub for sourcing
such agrochemicals. To bolster domestic production and reduce imports, the
Crop Care Federation of India (CCFI) has recommended specific measures to the
Government of India.[246]

### Transportation

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=24 "Edit
section: Transportation")]

#### Railways and Logistics

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=25 "Edit
section: Railways and Logistics")]

See also: [Indian Railways](/wiki/Indian_Railways "Indian Railways"), [Civil
aviation in India](/wiki/Civil_aviation_in_India "Civil aviation in India"),
and [Roads in India](/wiki/Roads_in_India "Roads in India")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Kalaburagi_-
_SMVT_Bengaluru_Vande_Bharat_Express.jpg/250px-Kalaburagi_-
_SMVT_Bengaluru_Vande_Bharat_Express.jpg)](/wiki/File:Kalaburagi_-
_SMVT_Bengaluru_Vande_Bharat_Express.jpg) [Vande
Bharat](/wiki/Vande_Bharat_\(trainset\) "Vande Bharat \(trainset\)") train

The [Indian Railways](/wiki/Indian_Railways "Indian Railways") contributes to
~3% of the country's gross domestic product (GDP) and has social obligations
pegged at $5.3 billion annually.[247] Indian Railways revenue has grown at 5%
CAGR in the past 5 years but profitability has reduced drastically in the past
4 years, due to growing infrastructure and modernization expenses. With a
workforce of 1.31 million people, the IR is also one of the country's largest
employers. The railways is a major contributor to jobs, GDP, and
mobility.[248]

Indian Railways has decided to revise its 2022–23 rolling stock production
plan upwards. The Ministry's new plan targets the production of 8,429 units
for the coming financial year. Production for 2022–23 has been raised by 878
units from the earlier planned 7,551, according to the revised targets.[249]
Indian Railways has targeted to manufacture 475 new [Vande
Bharat](/wiki/Vande_Bharat_\(trainset\) "Vande Bharat \(trainset\)") trainsets
for the next four years as a part of its modernization plan.[250] It is about
Rs 40,000 crore($5 billion) business opportunity that would also create 15,000
jobs and several spin-off benefits.[251] [Indian Railway's
CORE](/wiki/Central_Organisation_for_Railway_Electrification "Central
Organisation for Railway Electrification") aims to electrify all of its broad
gauge network by 31 March 2024.[252] The entire electrified mainline rail
network in India uses [25 kV AC](/wiki/25_kV_AC_railway_electrification "25 kV
AC railway electrification"); DC is used only for
[metros](/wiki/Urban_rail_transit_in_India "Urban rail transit in India"). As
of July 2023, India currently has 90% of total train tracks fully
electrified.[253]

Under the eleventh Five Year Plan of India (2007–12), the [Ministry of
Railways](/wiki/Ministry_of_Railways_\(India\) "Ministry of Railways
\(India\)") started constructing a new [Dedicated Freight
Corridor](/wiki/Dedicated_freight_corridors_in_India "Dedicated freight
corridors in India") (DFC) in two long routes, namely the
[Eastern](/wiki/Eastern_Dedicated_Freight_Corridor "Eastern Dedicated Freight
Corridor") and [Western](/wiki/Western_Dedicated_Freight_Corridor "Western
Dedicated Freight Corridor") freight corridors.[254] The two routes cover a
total length of 3,260 kilometres (2,030 mi), with the _Eastern Dedicated
Freight Corridor_ stretching from [Ludhiana](/wiki/Ludhiana "Ludhiana") in
[Punjab](/wiki/Punjab,_India "Punjab, India") to
[Dankuni](/wiki/Dankuni_railway_station "Dankuni railway station") in [West
Bengal](/wiki/West_Bengal "West Bengal") and the _Western Dedicated Freight
Corridor_ from [Jawaharlal Nehru Port](/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru_Port "Jawaharlal
Nehru Port") in [Mumbai](/wiki/Mumbai "Mumbai")
([Maharashtra](/wiki/Maharashtra "Maharashtra")) to [Dadri](/wiki/Dadri
"Dadri") in [Uttar Pradesh](/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh "Uttar Pradesh").[255] The DFC
will generate around 42,000 jobs and provide long term employment to many
people in public sector and private sector.[_[citation
needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

#### Rapid Transit

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=26 "Edit
section: Rapid Transit")]

Main article: [Urban rail transit in India](/wiki/Urban_rail_transit_in_India
"Urban rail transit in India")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Mumbai_Metro_at_Gundavali_Station.jpg/250px-
Mumbai_Metro_at_Gundavali_Station.jpg)](/wiki/File:Mumbai_Metro_at_Gundavali_Station.jpg)As
of 2024, India is having [third largest metro system in the
world](/wiki/List_of_metro_systems#List_of_countries_by_system_length "List of
metro systems")

India is developing modern [mass rapid transit](/wiki/Public_transport "Public
transport") systems to meet present and future urban requirements. A modern
metro rail system is already in place in the cities of [Navi
Mumbai](/wiki/Navi_Mumbai_Metro "Navi Mumbai Metro"),
[Delhi](/wiki/Delhi_Metro "Delhi Metro"), [Mumbai](/wiki/Mumbai_Metro "Mumbai
Metro"), [Bangalore](/wiki/Bangalore_Metro "Bangalore Metro"),
[Kolkata](/wiki/Kolkata_Metro "Kolkata Metro"),
[Hyderabad](/wiki/Hyderabad_Metro "Hyderabad Metro"),
[Kochi](/wiki/Kochi_Metro "Kochi Metro"), [Gurgaon](/wiki/Rapid_Metro_Gurgaon
"Rapid Metro Gurgaon"), [Jaipur](/wiki/Jaipur_Metro "Jaipur Metro"),
[Noida](/wiki/Noida_Metro "Noida Metro"), [Pune](/wiki/Pune_Metro "Pune
Metro"), [Nagpur](/wiki/Nagpur_Metro "Nagpur Metro"),
[Kanpur](/wiki/Kanpur_Metro "Kanpur Metro"), [Ahmedabad](/wiki/Ahmedabad_Metro
"Ahmedabad Metro") and [Lucknow](/wiki/Lucknow_Metro "Lucknow Metro"). Similar
mass transit systems are intended for [Agra](/wiki/Agra "Agra"),
[Bhopal](/wiki/Bhopal "Bhopal"), [Indore](/wiki/Indore "Indore"),
[Surat](/wiki/Surat "Surat"), [Patna](/wiki/Patna "Patna"), [Bhubaneswar Tri-
city](/wiki/Bhubaneswar "Bhubaneswar"), [Chandigarh Tri-city](/wiki/Chandigarh
"Chandigarh"), Gwalior, [Mysore](/wiki/Mysore "Mysore"),
[Nashik](/wiki/Nashik_Metro "Nashik Metro"), [Prayagraj](/wiki/Allahabad_Metro
"Allahabad Metro"), [Varanasi](/wiki/Varanasi_Metro "Varanasi Metro"),
[Ranchi](/wiki/Ranchi "Ranchi"), [Thane](/wiki/Thane "Thane") and
[Trivandrum](/wiki/Trivandrum "Trivandrum"). Former Prime Minister [Atal
Bihari Vajpayee](/wiki/Atal_Bihari_Vajpayee "Atal Bihari Vajpayee") has been
credited with success of the metro systems in India and every metro has
followed [Delhi Metro](/wiki/Delhi_Metro "Delhi Metro") model generating lot
of real estate wealth in India specially in smaller cities like
[Gurgaon](/wiki/Gurgaon "Gurgaon") and [Noida](/wiki/Noida "Noida"). For
Elevated corridor, there is no need for land acquisition as pillars are built
above [Median strip](/wiki/Median_strip "Median strip") of a
road.[256][_[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed
"Wikipedia:Citation needed")_] Land prices in tier-II cities such as Lucknow,
Patna, Jaipur, Ahmedabad, Pune, Kochi, and Coimbatore have gone up by almost
8-10 percent following the introduction of a metro corridor in these cities,
an assessment by JLL has said.[257]

India is also developing modern
[RRTS](/wiki/Delhi%E2%80%93Meerut_Regional_Rapid_Transit_System "Delhi–Meerut
Regional Rapid Transit System") system to replace the old
[MRTS](/wiki/Chennai_Mass_Rapid_Transit_System "Chennai Mass Rapid Transit
System") system which will provide connectivity in [Delhi Metropolitan
Area](/wiki/National_Capital_Region_\(India\) "National Capital Region
\(India\)") and [Mumbai Metropolitan Region](/wiki/Mumbai_Metropolitan_Region
"Mumbai Metropolitan Region") which will serve the suburbs of these big cities
at 80–100 km of distance from city centre.[_[citation
needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_]

#### Aviation

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=27 "Edit
section: Aviation")]

India is the fourth-largest civil aviation market in the world recording an
air traffic of 158 million passengers in 2017.[258][259] The market is
estimated to have 800 aircraft by 2020, which would account for 4.3% of global
volumes,[260] and is expected to record annual passenger traffic of 520
million by 2037.[259] [IATA](/wiki/IATA "IATA") estimated that aviation
contributed $30 billion to India's GDP in 2017, and supported 7.5 million jobs
– 390,000 directly, 570,000 in the value chain, and 6.2 million through
tourism.[259]

As of 2024, There are 75 new airports have been built in the last ten years,
taking the total count to 149 airports (include helipads and aerodromes).
Government vision is to take this milestone 149 to 220 airports in the next 5
to 7 years and [Government of India](/wiki/Government_of_India "Government of
India") has a Rs 1 lakh crore capex plan to spend on Airport
infrastructure.[261]

#### Shipbuilding

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=28 "Edit
section: Shipbuilding")]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Path_to_caves%2C_Gharapuri%2C_Maharashtra_400094%2C_India_-
_panoramio_%2814%29.jpg/250px-
Path_to_caves%2C_Gharapuri%2C_Maharashtra_400094%2C_India_-
_panoramio_%2814%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Path_to_caves,_Gharapuri,_Maharashtra_400094,_India_-
_panoramio_\(14\).jpg)_Maharishi Parshuram_ built in 2002 by Cochin Shipyard
for SCI

The Shipbuilding Financial Assistance Policy (SBFAP) was introduced in 2016,
provides financial assistance to Indian shipyards for shipbuilding contracts.
According to the ministry of ports, shipping and waterways, under SBFAP, a
total of 313 domestic and export vessel orders were procured by 39 shipyards
since the inception of the scheme, with the total value standing at about
₹10,500 crore($1.26 billion).[262]

India has multiple ship building companies such as [Cochin
Shipyard](/wiki/Cochin_Shipyard "Cochin Shipyard"), [Hindustan
Shipyard](/wiki/Hindustan_Shipyard "Hindustan Shipyard") and [Swan Defence and
Heavy Industries](/wiki/Swan_Defence_and_Heavy_Industries_Limited "Swan
Defence and Heavy Industries Limited"), mainly produces ships for European,
South American and African shipping companies. Cochin shipyard is the pioneer
in autonomous electric propulsion ships.[263]

### IT and telecommunications

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=29 "Edit
section: IT and telecommunications")]

#### Datacentre and cloud

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=30 "Edit
section: Datacentre and cloud")]

Main article: [Data centre industry in
India](/wiki/Data_centre_industry_in_India "Data centre industry in India")

India has emerged as the leading data centre hub in the Asia-Pacific region
(excluding China), surpassing established players like Singapore, Australia,
South Korea, Japan, and Hong Kong in installed capacity. This reflects the
escalating demand for data services in one of the world's fastest-growing
major economies. With a current installed capacity of 950 MW and projections
indicating an additional 850 MW by 2026, India is poised to solidify its
position as a key player in the Asia-Pacific data centre landscape.[264]

In Hyderabad, The largest datacentre is spread over 1. 31 lakh square feet,
the proposed rated-4 (tier-4) data centre will be equipped with 1,600 racks
and powered by 18MW of electricity.[265]

India's overall public cloud services market is projected to hit US $13
billion by 2026, expanding at a CAGR of 23.1% in 2021-26. The market's revenue
totaled US $2.8 billion for the first half of 2022.[266]

#### Telecommunications

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=31 "Edit
section: Telecommunications")]

Main article: [Telecommunications in India](/wiki/Telecommunications_in_India
"Telecommunications in India")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/STS008-44-611.jpg/250px-
STS008-44-611.jpg)](/wiki/File:STS008-44-611.jpg)[INSAT-1B](/wiki/INSAT-1B
"INSAT-1B") [satellite](/wiki/Satellite "Satellite"): broadcasting sector in
India is highly dependent on [INSAT](/wiki/INSAT "INSAT") system.

The telecommunication sector generated ₹2.20 trillion (US$26 billion) in
revenue in 2014–15, accounting for 1.94% of total GDP.[267] Telecom equipment
manufacturing and exports are becoming a new success story following the
success of smartphone exports. In the financial year 2024, the telecom
equipment production exceeded the milestone of Rs 45,000 crore with exports
totaling approximately Rs 10,500 crore.[268] India is the second-largest
market in the world by number of telephone users (both fixed and mobile
phones) with 1.053 billion subscribers as of 31 August
2016.[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit)
It has one of the lowest call-tariffs in the world, due to fierce competition
among telecom operators. India has the world's third-largest Internet user-
base. As of 31 March
2016,[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit)
there were 342.65 million Internet subscribers in the country.[269] India's
[telecommunication industry](/wiki/Telecommunications_in_India
"Telecommunications in India") is the world's second largest by the number of
mobile phone, smartphone, and internet users. It is the world's [24th-largest
oil producer](/wiki/List_of_countries_by_oil_production "List of countries by
oil production") and the [third-largest oil
consumer](/wiki/List_of_countries_by_oil_consumption "List of countries by oil
consumption").[270]

Industry estimates indicate that there are over 554 million TV consumers in
India as of
2012.[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit)[271]
India is the largest [direct-to-home](/wiki/Direct-to-home "Direct-to-home")
(DTH) television market in the world by number of subscribers. As of May
2016,[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit)
there were 84.80 million [DTH](/wiki/Satellite_television "Satellite
television") subscribers in the country.[272]

### Defence and energy

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=32 "Edit
section: Defence and energy")]

#### Defence

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=33 "Edit
section: Defence")]

Main article: [Defence industry of India](/wiki/Defence_industry_of_India
"Defence industry of India")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/DRDO_AIP_%28Air_Independent_Propulsion%29_model_for_Kalvari-
class_submarine.jpg/250px-
DRDO_AIP_%28Air_Independent_Propulsion%29_model_for_Kalvari-
class_submarine.jpg)](/wiki/File:DRDO_AIP_\(Air_Independent_Propulsion\)_model_for_Kalvari-
class_submarine.jpg)PAFC AIP fuel-cell module developed by the
[DRDO](/wiki/Defence_Research_and_Development_Organisation "Defence Research
and Development Organisation") of [India](/wiki/India "India") to power marine
transport

With over 1.3 million active personnel, the [Indian Army](/wiki/Indian_Army
"Indian Army") is the [third-largest military force and the largest volunteer
army](/wiki/List_of_countries_by_number_of_military_and_paramilitary_personnel
"List of countries by number of military and paramilitary personnel"). Defence
expenditure was pegged at US$70.12 billion for fiscal year 2022–23 and,
increased 9.8% than previous fiscal year.[273] India is the world's second
largest arms importer; between 2016 and 2020, it accounted for 9.5% of the
total [global arms imports](/wiki/Arms_industry "Arms industry").[274] India
exported military hardware worth ₹159.2 billion (US$1.9 billion) in the
financial year 2022–23, the highest ever and a notable tenfold increase since
2016–17.[275]

#### Diversified Energy sector

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=34 "Edit
section: Diversified Energy sector")]

Main articles: [Electricity sector in India](/wiki/Electricity_sector_in_India
"Electricity sector in India") and [Energy policy of
India](/wiki/Energy_policy_of_India "Energy policy of India")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/PHWR_under_Construction_at_Kakrapar_Gujarat_India.jpg/250px-
PHWR_under_Construction_at_Kakrapar_Gujarat_India.jpg)](/wiki/File:PHWR_under_Construction_at_Kakrapar_Gujarat_India.jpg)An
[IPHWR-700](/wiki/IPHWR-700 "IPHWR-700") reactor undergoing construction in
[Kakrapar Nuclear Plant](/wiki/Kakrapar_Atomic_Power_Station "Kakrapar Atomic
Power Station") started in 1984

[Primary energy](/wiki/Primary_energy "Primary energy") consumption of India
is the third-largest after China and US with 5.3% global share in the year
2015.[276] Coal and crude oil together account for 85% of the primary energy
consumption of India. India's [oil reserves](/wiki/Oil_reserves "Oil
reserves") meet 25% of the country's domestic oil demand.[277][278] As of
April
2015,[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit)
India's total proven crude oil reserves are 763.476 megatonnes (751.418
million long tons; 841.588 million short tons), while gas reserves stood at
1,490 billion cubic metres (53 trillion cubic feet).[279] Oil and natural gas
fields are located offshore at [Ashoknagar Oil
Field](/wiki/Ashoknagar_Oil_Field "Ashoknagar Oil Field"), [Bombay
High](/wiki/Bombay_High "Bombay High"), [Krishna Godavari
Basin](/wiki/Krishna_Godavari_Basin "Krishna Godavari Basin"), [Mangala
Area](/wiki/Mangala_Area "Mangala Area") and the [Cauvery
Delta](/wiki/Cauvery_Delta "Cauvery Delta"), and onshore mainly in the states
of [West Bengal](/wiki/West_Bengal "West Bengal"), [Assam](/wiki/Assam
"Assam"), Gujarat and [Rajasthan](/wiki/Rajasthan "Rajasthan"). India is the
fourth-largest consumer of oil and net oil imports were nearly ₹8.2 trillion
(US$97 billion) in 2014–15,[279] which had an adverse effect on the country's
[current account deficit](/wiki/Current_account_deficit "Current account
deficit"). The petroleum industry in India mostly consists of public sector
companies such as [Oil and Natural Gas
Corporation](/wiki/Oil_and_Natural_Gas_Corporation "Oil and Natural Gas
Corporation") (ONGC), [Hindustan Petroleum Corporation
Limited](/wiki/Hindustan_Petroleum_Corporation_Limited "Hindustan Petroleum
Corporation Limited") (HPCL), [Bharat Petroleum Corporation
Limited](/wiki/Bharat_Petroleum_Corporation_Limited "Bharat Petroleum
Corporation Limited") (BPCL) and Indian Oil Corporation Limited (IOCL). There
are some major private Indian companies in the oil sector such as [Reliance
Industries Limited](/wiki/Reliance_Industries_Limited "Reliance Industries
Limited") (RIL) which operates the world's largest oil refining complex.[280]

India became the world's third-largest producer of electricity in 2013 with a
4.8% global share in electricity generation, surpassing Japan and Russia.[281]
By the end of calendar year 2015, India had an electricity surplus with many
power stations idling for want of demand.[282] The utility electricity sector
had an installed capacity of 303 [GW](/wiki/Gigawatt "Gigawatt") as of May
2016[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit)
of which [thermal power](/wiki/Thermal_power "Thermal power") contributed
69.8%, [hydroelectricity](/wiki/Hydroelectricity "Hydroelectricity") 15.2%,
other sources of [renewable energy](/wiki/Renewable_energy "Renewable energy")
13.0%, and [nuclear power](/wiki/Nuclear_power "Nuclear power") 2.1%.[283]
India meets most of its domestic electricity demand through its 106 gigatonnes
(104 billion long tons; 117 billion short tons) of proven coal reserves.[284]
India is also rich in certain alternative [sources of
energy](/wiki/Energy_policy_of_India "Energy policy of India") with
significant future potential such as [solar](/wiki/Solar_power_in_India "Solar
power in India"), [wind](/wiki/Wind_power_in_India "Wind power in India") and
[biofuels](/wiki/Biofuel_in_India "Biofuel in India")
([jatropha](/wiki/Jatropha "Jatropha"), sugarcane). India's dwindling uranium
reserves stagnated the growth of nuclear energy in the country for many
years.[285] Recent discoveries in the
[Tummalapalle](/wiki/Tummalapalle_uranium_mine "Tummalapalle uranium mine")
belt may be among the top 20 natural uranium reserves
worldwide,[286][287][288][_[needs
update](/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items
"Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers")_] and an estimated reserve of
846,477 tonnes (833,108 long tons; 933,081 short tons) of
[thorium](/wiki/Thorium "Thorium")[289] – about 25% of world's reserves – are
expected to fuel the country's ambitious [nuclear energy
programme](/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_India "Nuclear power in India") in the long
run. The [Indo-US nuclear
deal](/wiki/India%E2%80%93United_States_Civil_Nuclear_Agreement "India–United
States Civil Nuclear Agreement") has also paved the way for India to import
uranium from other countries.[290]

### Infrastructure sector

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=35 "Edit
section: Infrastructure sector")]

![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/60px-
Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png)| This article needs to be **updated**.
Please help update this article to reflect recent events or newly available
information. _( June 2023)_  
---|---  
  
Main articles: [Roads in India](/wiki/Roads_in_India "Roads in India"),
[Indian Railways](/wiki/Indian_Railways "Indian Railways"), [Ports in
India](/wiki/Ports_in_India "Ports in India"), and [Construction industry of
India](/wiki/Construction_industry_of_India "Construction industry of India")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/MSC_Claude_at_Vizhinjam.jpg/250px-
MSC_Claude_at_Vizhinjam.jpg)](/wiki/File:MSC_Claude_at_Vizhinjam.jpg)[Vizhinjam
International Seaport
Thiruvananthapuram](/wiki/Vizhinjam_International_Seaport_Thiruvananthapuram
"Vizhinjam International Seaport Thiruvananthapuram"), the first _Mother Port_
in the world, with harbor depth of 24 metres

India's infrastructure and transport sector contributes about 5% of its GDP.
India has a [road network](/wiki/Road_network "Road network") of over
5,472,144 kilometres (3,400,233 mi) as of 31 March
2015,[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit)
the [second-largest road network](/wiki/List_of_countries_by_road_network_size
"List of countries by road network size") in the world only behind United
States. At 1.66 km of roads per square kilometre of land (2.68 miles per
square mile), the quantitative density of India's road network is higher than
that of Japan (0.91) and United States (0.67), and far higher than that of
China (0.46), Brazil (0.18) or Russia (0.08).[291] Qualitatively, India's
roads are a mix of modern highways and narrow, unpaved roads, and are being
improved.[292] As of 31 March
2015,[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit)
87.05% of Indian roads were paved.[291] It is upgrading its infrastructure. As
of May
2014,[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit)
India had completed over 22,600 kilometres (14,000 mi) of 4- or 6-lane
highways, connecting most of its major manufacturing, commercial and cultural
centres.[293] India's road infrastructure carries 60% of freight and 87% of
passenger traffic.[294]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Delhi-Meerut-Express-
Highway-India.png/250px-Delhi-Meerut-Express-Highway-
India.png)](/wiki/File:Delhi-Meerut-Express-Highway-India.png)[Delhi-Meerut
Expressway](/wiki/Delhi-Meerut_Expressway "Delhi-Meerut Expressway") is the
2nd widest 14 lane [expressway](/wiki/Expressways_of_India "Expressways of
India") after [Dwarka Expressway](/wiki/Dwarka_Expressway "Dwarka Expressway")

India has a coastline of 7,500 kilometres (4,700 mi) with 13 major ports, 15
big private ports and 60 operational non-major ports, which together handle
95% of the country's external trade by volume and 70% by value (most of the
remainder handled by air).[295] [Kandla Port](/wiki/Kandla_Port "Kandla
Port"), [New Kandla](/wiki/Kandla "Kandla") is the largest public port
established in early 1960's, while [Mundra](/wiki/Mundra_Port "Mundra Port")
is the largest private sea port.[296] The airport infrastructure of India
includes 125 airports,[297] of which 66 airports are licensed to handle both
passengers and cargo.[298] India has multiple global infrastructure companies
such as [Afcons Infrastructure](/wiki/Afcons_Infrastructure "Afcons
Infrastructure"), [Adani Group](/wiki/Adani_Ports_%26_SEZ "Adani Ports &
SEZ"), [JSW Infrastructure](/wiki/JSW_Group "JSW Group"), [Larsen &
Toubro](/wiki/Larsen_%26_Toubro "Larsen & Toubro") etc.

The construction industry contributed $288 billion (13% of GDP) and employed
60.42 million people (14% of the workforce) in 2016.[243] The
[construction](/wiki/Construction_industry_of_India "Construction industry of
India") and real estate sector ranks third among the 14 major sectors in terms
of direct, indirect, and induced effects in all sectors of the economy.[299]

The real estate sector will provide huge business opportunities, employment
and big avenues for startup ecosystem. The [2023 Union budget of
India](/wiki/2023_Union_budget_of_India "2023 Union budget of India") also
focused significantly on infrastructure with nearly ₹10 trillion direct
investment of central government.[300]

India has [MGNREGA](/wiki/MGNREGA "MGNREGA") scheme which provides employment
to 80 million people and as well as provides large infrastructure
development.[19] India is massively privatizing [airports](/wiki/Airports
"Airports"), ports, [bus stands](/wiki/Inter-State_Bus_Terminals "Inter-State
Bus Terminals"), [railway stations](/wiki/Railway_stations "Railway
stations"), [dams](/wiki/Dams "Dams"), [dam wind park](/wiki/Wind_park "Wind
park"),[301] [solar parks](/wiki/Photovoltaic_power_station "Photovoltaic
power station"), [floating solar plants](/wiki/Floating_solar "Floating
solar"), [power transmission](/wiki/Power_transmission "Power transmission"),
[highways](/wiki/Highways "Highways"), [thermal power](/wiki/Thermal_power
"Thermal power") and other utilizes.

### Finance and trade

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=36 "Edit
section: Finance and trade")]

#### Banking and financial services

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=37 "Edit
section: Banking and financial services")]

Main articles: [Banking in India](/wiki/Banking_in_India "Banking in India")
and [Finance in India](/wiki/Finance_in_India "Finance in India")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/BKC_-
_panoramio.jpg/250px-BKC_-_panoramio.jpg)](/wiki/File:BKC_-
_panoramio.jpg)[Bank of India](/wiki/Bank_of_India "Bank of India") HQ in
[BKC](/wiki/Bandra_Kurla_Complex "Bandra Kurla Complex")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/India_bonds.webp/250px-
India_bonds.webp.png)](/wiki/File:India_bonds.webp)India bonds

  30 year

  10 year

  2 year

  1 year

  3 month

The financial services industry contributed $809 billion (37% of GDP) and
employed 14.17 million people (3% of the workforce) in 2016, and the banking
sector contributed $407 billion (19% of GDP) and employed 5.5 million people
(1% of the workforce) in 2016.[243] The Indian [money
market](/wiki/Money_market "Money market") is classified into the organised
sector, comprising private, public and foreign-owned [commercial
banks](/wiki/Commercial_bank "Commercial bank") and
[cooperative](/wiki/Cooperative "Cooperative") banks, together known as
'scheduled banks'; and the unorganised sector, which includes individual or
family-owned indigenous bankers or [money lenders](/wiki/Money_lending "Money
lending") and [non-banking financial companies](/wiki/Non-
banking_financial_company "Non-banking financial company").[302] The
unorganised sector and [microcredit](/wiki/Microcredit "Microcredit") are
preferred over traditional banks in rural and sub-urban areas, especially for
non-productive purposes such as short-term loans for ceremonies.[303]

Prime Minister [Indira Gandhi](/wiki/Indira_Gandhi "Indira Gandhi")
nationalised 14 banks in 1969, followed by six others in 1980, and made it
mandatory for banks to provide 40% of their net credit to priority sectors
including agriculture, small-scale industry, retail trade and small business,
to ensure that the banks fulfilled their social and developmental goals. Since
then, the number of bank branches has increased from 8,260 in 1969 to 72,170
in 2007 and the population covered by a branch decreased from 63,800 to 15,000
during the same period. The total [bank deposits](/wiki/Bank_deposit "Bank
deposit") increased from ₹59.1 billion (equivalent to ₹2.8 trillion or US$33
billion in 2023) in 1970–71 to ₹38.31 trillion (equivalent to ₹96 trillion or
US$1.1 trillion in 2023) in 2008–09. Despite an increase of rural branches –
from 1,860 or 22% of the total in 1969 to 30,590 or 42% in 2007 – only 32,270
of 500,000 villages are served by a scheduled bank.[304][305]

India's gross domestic [savings](/wiki/Saving "Saving") in 2006–07 as a
percentage of GDP stood at a high 32.8%.[306] More than half of personal
savings are invested in physical assets such as land, houses, cattle, and
gold.[307] The government-owned public-sector banks hold over 75% of total
assets of the banking industry, with the private and foreign banks holding
18.2% and 6.5% respectively.[57] Since liberalisation, the government has
approved significant banking reforms. While some of these relate to
nationalised banks – such as reforms encouraging mergers, reducing government
interference and increasing profitability and competitiveness – other reforms
have opened the banking and insurance sectors to private and foreign
companies.[277][308]

#### Retail

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=38 "Edit
section: Retail")]

Main article: [Retailing in India](/wiki/Retailing_in_India "Retailing in
India")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Surat_Diamond_Bourse_Aerial_View_1.jpg/250px-
Surat_Diamond_Bourse_Aerial_View_1.jpg)](/wiki/File:Surat_Diamond_Bourse_Aerial_View_1.jpg)Surat
Diamond City Aerial View

The retail industry, excluding wholesale, contributed $793 billion (10% of
GDP) and employed 35 million people (8% of the workforce) in 2020. The
industry is the second largest employer in India, after
agriculture.[309][310][311] The Indian retail market is estimated to be US$600
billion and one of the top-five retail markets in the world by economic value.
India has one of the fastest-growing retail markets in the world,[312][313]
and is projected to reach $1.3 trillion by 2020.[314][315] India has [retail
market](/wiki/Retailing_in_India "Retailing in India") worth $1.17 trillion,
which contributes over 10% of India's GDP. It also has one of the world's
fastest growing [e-commerce](/wiki/E-commerce "E-commerce") markets.[316] The
e-commerce retail market in India was valued at $32.7 billion in 2018, and is
expected to reach $71.9 billion by 2022.[317]

India's retail industry mostly consists of local [mom-and-pop
stores](/wiki/Small_business "Small business"), owner-staffed shops and street
vendors. Retail supermarkets are expanding, with a market share of 4% in
2008.[318] In 2012, the government permitted 51% FDI in multi-brand retail and
100% FDI in single-brand retail. However, a lack of back-end warehouse
infrastructure and state-level permits and red tape continue to limit growth
of organised retail.[319] Compliance with over thirty regulations such as
"signboard licences" and "anti-hoarding measures" must be made before a store
can open for business. There are taxes for moving goods from state to state,
and even within states.[318]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Harangi_Elephant_Camp_and_Tree_Park.jpg/250px-
Harangi_Elephant_Camp_and_Tree_Park.jpg)](/wiki/File:Harangi_Elephant_Camp_and_Tree_Park.jpg)
Scenic view from [Harangi Elephant Camp & Tree
Park](/wiki/Harangi_Dam#Amenities "Harangi Dam") in [Coorg](/wiki/Kodagu
"Kodagu")

### Industries

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=39 "Edit
section: Industries")]

#### Tourism

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=40 "Edit
section: Tourism")]

Main article: [Tourism in India](/wiki/Tourism_in_India "Tourism in India")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Sarayu_River_night_view%2C_Ayodhya_001.jpg/250px-
Sarayu_River_night_view%2C_Ayodhya_001.jpg)](/wiki/File:Sarayu_River_night_view,_Ayodhya_001.jpg)[Ayodhya](/wiki/Ayodhya
"Ayodhya") became the top destination in 2024

The [World Travel & Tourism Council](/wiki/World_Travel_and_Tourism_Council
"World Travel and Tourism Council") calculated that tourism generated ₹15.24
trillion (US$180 billion) or 9.4% of the nation's GDP in 2017 and supported
41.622 million jobs, 8% of its total employment. The sector is predicted to
grow at an annual rate of 6.9% to ₹32.05 trillion (US$380 billion) by 2028
(9.9% of GDP).[320] Over 10 million foreign tourists arrived in India in 2017
compared to 8.89 million in 2016, recording a growth of 15.6%.[321] The
[tourism industry](/wiki/Tourism_in_India "Tourism in India") contributes
about 9.2% of India's GDP and employs over 42 million people.[322] India
earned $21.07 billion in foreign exchange from tourism receipts in 2015.[323]
International tourism to India has seen a steady growth from 2.37 million
arrivals in 1997 to 8.03 million arrivals in 2015.
[Bangladesh](/wiki/Bangladesh "Bangladesh") is the largest source of
international tourists to India, while European Union nations and Japan are
other major sources of international tourists.[324][325] In 2024,
[Ayodhya](/wiki/Ayodhya "Ayodhya"), particularly with the [Ram
Temple](/wiki/Ram_Mandir "Ram Mandir") became Uttar Pradesh's top tourist
destination, attracting more domestic and international visitors.[326]
[Coorg](/wiki/Kodagu_district "Kodagu district") became the top fastest
growing destination and hill station in 2024.[327][328] Less than 10% of
international tourists visit the [Taj Mahal](/wiki/Taj_Mahal "Taj Mahal"),
with the majority visiting other cultural, thematic and holiday circuits.[329]
Over 12 million Indian citizens take international trips each year for
tourism, while domestic tourism within India adds about 740 million Indian
travellers.[324] India has a fast-growing [medical tourism
sector](/wiki/Medical_tourism_in_India "Medical tourism in India") of its
health care economy, offering low-cost health services and long-term
care.[330][331] In October 2015, the medical tourism sector was estimated to
be worth US$3 billion. It is projected to grow to $7–8 billion by 2020.[332]
In 2014, 184,298 foreign patients traveled to India to seek medical
treatment.[333]

#### Wedding Industry

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=41 "Edit
section: Wedding Industry")]

India's wedding industry has a size of $75 billion, second largest after
China. It is the fourth largest industry in India.[334] The wedding industry
saw significant growth in 2023 as weddings became larger and more extravagant
post the pandemic blues. Average guest size grew by nearly 15%, from 270
guests in 2022 to 310 in 2023. Celebrations also expanded, with couples now
hosting an average of 4.2 functions, up from 3.2.A significant 27 lakh
weddings fall in the ₹10-25 lakh range, reflecting the popularity of mid-range
wedding budgets. Smaller budgets like ₹3 lakh and ₹6 lakh account for 10 lakh
weddings each, while high-end weddings costing ₹1 crore or more make up 50,000
weddings.

India hosts the highest number of weddings in the world each year, as per
investment banking and capital market firm Jefferies. However, in terms of
market size, the Indian wedding industry is still smaller than China's but
almost twice as big as the US's.[335]

#### Films, entertainment and music industry

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=42 "Edit
section: Films, entertainment and music industry")]

Main article: [Cinema of India](/wiki/Cinema_of_India "Cinema of India")

The Indian cinema industry is expected to garner a revenue of around Rs 16,198
crore by 2026, of which Rs 15,849 would be Box office revenue and the rest Rs
349 crore from advertising, the report added.[336] India's Recorded Music
industry (which is a key sub-segment) is making steady progress at a CAGR of
13.6 percent, thanks to streaming models.[336]

## Regional economies

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=43 "Edit
section: Regional economies")]

Further information: [List of Indian states and union territories by
GDP](/wiki/List_of_Indian_states_and_union_territories_by_GDP "List of Indian
states and union territories by GDP")

India's regional economies vary significantly in terms of output, industrial
composition, and development levels. [Maharashtra](/wiki/Maharashtra
"Maharashtra") has the largest state economy, contributing over 13% to the
national GDP, followed by [Tamil Nadu](/wiki/Tamil_Nadu "Tamil Nadu"),
[Karnataka](/wiki/Karnataka "Karnataka"), [Gujarat](/wiki/Gujarat "Gujarat"),
and [Uttar Pradesh](/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh "Uttar Pradesh").[337] These five
states collectively account for nearly half of India's economic output.
[Southern states](/wiki/South_India "South India") such as Tamil Nadu,
Karnataka, and [Telangana](/wiki/Telangana "Telangana") are known for strong
performance in services and manufacturing, while western states like Gujarat
and Maharashtra are industrial powerhouses. In contrast, eastern and central
states such as [Bihar](/wiki/Bihar "Bihar"), [Jharkhand](/wiki/Jharkhand
"Jharkhand"), and [Chhattisgarh](/wiki/Chhattisgarh "Chhattisgarh") exhibit
lower GDPs, reflecting ongoing challenges in industrialisation and
infrastructure. Union Territories like [Delhi](/wiki/Delhi "Delhi") and
[Chandigarh](/wiki/Chandigarh "Chandigarh"), though smaller in size, have high
per capita incomes due to urban-centric service-driven economies. This
economic landscape highlights both growth opportunities and regional
disparities, underscoring the need for tailored economic strategies across
states.

Percent of share of states in GDP of India in 2024-25

  1. [Maharashtra](/wiki/Maharashtra "Maharashtra") (13.17%)
  2. [Tamil Nadu](/wiki/Tamil_Nadu "Tamil Nadu") (9.56%)
  3. [Karnataka](/wiki/Karnataka "Karnataka") (8.68%)
  4. [Gujarat](/wiki/Gujarat "Gujarat") (8.64%)
  5. [Uttar Pradesh](/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh "Uttar Pradesh") (8.22%)
  6. [West Bengal](/wiki/West_Bengal "West Bengal") (5.79%)
  7. [Rajasthan](/wiki/Rajasthan "Rajasthan") (5.29%)
  8. [Andhra Pradesh](/wiki/Andhra_Pradesh "Andhra Pradesh") (4.66%)
  9. [Telangana](/wiki/Telangana "Telangana") (4.71%)
  10. [Madhya Pradesh](/wiki/Madhya_Pradesh "Madhya Pradesh") (4.67%)
  11. [Kerala](/wiki/Kerala "Kerala") (3.8%)
  12. Others (22.81%)

## Employment

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=44 "Edit
section: Employment")]

See also: [Labour in India](/wiki/Labour_in_India "Labour in India"), [Indian
labour law](/wiki/Indian_labour_law "Indian labour law"), [Child labour in
India](/wiki/Child_labour_in_India "Child labour in India"), and [Unemployment
in India](/wiki/Unemployment_in_India "Unemployment in India")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Share-of-population-
in-extreme-poverty.png/500px-Share-of-population-in-extreme-
poverty.png)](/wiki/File:Share-of-population-in-extreme-poverty.png)India:
extreme poverty rates from 1977 to 2021[339]

Agricultural and allied sectors accounted for about 52% of the total workforce
in 2009–10. While agriculture employment has fallen over time in percentage of
labour employed, services which include construction and infrastructure have
seen a steady growth accounting for 20.3% of employment in 2012–13.[340] Of
the total workforce, 7% is in the organised sector, two-thirds of which are in
the government-controlled public sector.[341] About 51.2% of the workforce in
India is self-employed.[340] Despite having one of the longest working hours,
India has one of the lowest [workforce
productivity](/wiki/Workforce_productivity "Workforce productivity") levels in
the world.[342][343][344][345][346] According to a 2005–06 survey, there is a
gender gap in employment and salaries. In rural areas, both men and women are
primarily self-employed, mostly in agriculture. In urban areas, salaried work
was the largest source of employment for both men and women in 2006.[347]

### Unemployment

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=45 "Edit
section: Unemployment")]

Main article: [Unemployment in India](/wiki/Unemployment_in_India
"Unemployment in India")

Unemployment in India is characterised by [chronic (disguised)
unemployment](/wiki/Unemployment_types#Hidden_unemployment "Unemployment
types"). Government schemes that target eradication of both poverty and
unemployment – which in recent decades has sent millions of poor and unskilled
people into urban areas in search of livelihoods – attempt to solve the
problem by providing financial assistance for starting businesses, honing
skills, setting up public sector enterprises, reservations in governments,
etc. The decline in organised employment, due to the decreased role of the
public sector after liberalisation, has further underlined the need for
focusing on better education and created political pressure for further
reforms.[348][349] India's labour regulations are heavy, even by developing
country standards, and analysts have urged the government to abolish or modify
them to make the environment more conducive for employment
generation.[350][351] The [11th five-year plan](/wiki/Eleventh_Five-
Year_Plan_\(India\) "Eleventh Five-Year Plan \(India\)") has also identified
the need for a congenial environment to be created for employment generation,
by reducing the number of permissions and other bureaucratic clearances
required.[352] Inequalities and inadequacies in the education system have been
identified as an obstacle, which prevents the benefits of increased employment
opportunities from reaching all sectors of society.[353]

### Child labour

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=46 "Edit
section: Child labour")]

Main article: [Child labour in India](/wiki/Child_labour_in_India "Child
labour in India")

Child labour is a complex problem that is rooted in poverty. Since the 1990s,
the government has implemented a variety of programs to eliminate child
labour. These have included setting up schools, launching free school lunch
programs, creating special investigation cells, etc.[354][355] Author Sonalde
Desai stated that recent studies on child labour in India have found some
pockets of industries in which children are employed, but overall, relatively
few Indian children are employed. Child labour below the age of 10 is now
rare. In the 10–14 age group, the latest surveys find only 2% of children
working for wage, while another 9% work within their home or rural farms
assisting their parents in times of high work demand such as sowing and
harvesting of crops.[356]

### Diaspora remittance

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=47 "Edit
section: Diaspora remittance")]

India has the largest diaspora around the world, an estimated 32 million
people according to the [Ministry of External Affairs
(India)](/wiki/Ministry_of_External_Affairs_\(India\) "Ministry of External
Affairs \(India\)"),[357] many of whom work overseas and remit funds back to
their families. The Middle East region is the largest source of employment for
expat Indians. The crude oil production and infrastructure industry of Saudi
Arabia employs over 2 million expat Indians. Cities such as
[Dubai](/wiki/Dubai "Dubai") and [Abu Dhabi](/wiki/Abu_Dhabi "Abu Dhabi") in
United Arab Emirates have employed another 2 million Indians during the
construction boom in recent decades.[358] In 2009–10,
[remittances](/wiki/Remittances_to_India "Remittances to India") from Indian
migrants overseas stood at [₹](/wiki/Indian_rupee "Indian rupee")2.5
[trillion](/wiki/1,000,000,000,000 "1,000,000,000,000") (equivalent to ₹5.6
trillion or US$66 billion in 2023), the highest in the world, but their share
in FDI remained low at around 1%.[359]

### Trade unions

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=48 "Edit
section: Trade unions")]

Main article: [Trade unions in India](/wiki/Trade_unions_in_India "Trade
unions in India")

In India, the Trade Union movement is generally divided on political lines.
According to provisional statistics from the [Ministry of
Labour](/wiki/Ministry_of_Labour_and_Employment_\(India\) "Ministry of Labour
and Employment \(India\)"), trade unions had a combined membership of
24,601,589 in 2002. As of 2008, there are 12 Central Trade Union Organisations
(CTUO) recognized by the Ministry of Labour.[360] The forming of these unions
was a big deal in India. It led to a big push for more regulatory laws which
gave workers a lot more power.[361]

[AITUC](/wiki/AITUC "AITUC") is the oldest trade union in India. It is a left
supported organization. A trade union with nearly 2,000,000 members is the
Self Employed Women's Association (SEWA) which protects the rights of Indian
women working in the informal economy. In addition to the protection of
rights, SEWA educates, mobilizes, finances, and exalts their members'
trades.[362] Multiple other organizations represent workers. These
organizations are formed upon different political groups. These different
groups allow different groups of people with different political views to join
a Union.[363]

## Income and consumption

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=49 "Edit
section: Income and consumption")]

Main article: [Income in India](/wiki/Income_in_India "Income in India")

![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/60px-
Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png)| This article needs to be **updated**.
Please help update this article to reflect recent events or newly available
information. _( March 2022)_  
---|---  
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/India%27s_GDP_per_capital_nominal_compared_to_other_countries_in_2025.png/500px-
India%27s_GDP_per_capital_nominal_compared_to_other_countries_in_2025.png)](/wiki/File:India%27s_GDP_per_capital_nominal_compared_to_other_countries_in_2025.png)India
vs world by nominal GDP per capita in 2025[364]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/GINI_index_World_Bank_up_to_2018.png/330px-
GINI_index_World_Bank_up_to_2018.png)](/wiki/File:GINI_index_World_Bank_up_to_2018.png)[Gini
index](/wiki/Gini_coefficient "Gini coefficient") of India compared to other
countries per [World Bank](/wiki/World_Bank "World Bank") data tables as of
2018[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit)[365]

India's [gross national income](/wiki/Gross_national_income "Gross national
income") per capita had experienced high growth rates since 2002. It tripled
from ₹19,040 in 2002–03 to ₹53,331 in 2010–11, averaging 13.7% growth each of
these eight years, with peak growth of 15.6% in 2010–11[366] and, growth in
the inflation-adjusted per-capita income of the nation slowed to 5.6% in
2010–11, down from 6.4% in the previous year. These consumption levels are on
an individual basis.[367] The average family income in India was $6,671 per
household in 2011.[368]

According to 2011 census data, India has about 330 million houses and 247
million households. The household size in India has dropped in recent years,
the 2011 census reporting 50% of households have four or fewer members, with
an average of 4.8 members per household including surviving
grandparents.[369][370] These households produced a GDP of about $1.7
trillion.[371] Consumption patterns note: approximately 67% of households use
firewood, crop residue, or cow-dung cakes for cooking purposes; 53% do not
have sanitation or drainage facilities on premises; 83% have water supply
within their premises or 100 metres (330 ft) from their house in urban areas
and 500 metres (1,600 ft) from the house in rural areas; 67% of the households
have access to electricity; 63% of households have landline or mobile
telephone service; 43% have a television; 26% have either a two- or four-wheel
motor vehicle. Compared to 2001, these income and consumption trends represent
moderate to significant improvements.[369] One report in 2010 claimed that
high-income households outnumber low-income households.[372]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Countries_by_GNI_%28nominal%2C_Atlas_method%29_per_capita_in_2016.png/330px-
Countries_by_GNI_%28nominal%2C_Atlas_method%29_per_capita_in_2016.png)](/wiki/File:Countries_by_GNI_\(nominal,_Atlas_method\)_per_capita_in_2016.png)Countries
by nominal GNI per capita according to the [Atlas method](/wiki/Atlas_method
"Atlas method") (2016)[373]

New World Wealth publishes reports tracking the total wealth of countries,
which is measured as the private wealth held by all residents of a country.
According to New World Wealth, India's total wealth increased from $3,165
billion in 2007 to $8,230 billion in 2017, a growth rate of 160%. India's
total wealth decreased by 1% from $8.23 trillion in 2017 to $8.148 trillion in
2018, making it the sixth wealthiest nation in the world. There are 20,730
multimillionaires (7th largest in the world)[374] and 118 billionaires in
India (3rd largest in the world). With 327,100 high net-worth individuals
(HNWI), India is home to the 9th highest number of HNWIs in the world. Mumbai
is the wealthiest Indian city and the 12th wealthiest in the world, with a
total net worth of $941 billion in 2018. Twenty-eight billionaires reside in
the city, ranked ninth worldwide.[375] As of December
2016,[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit)
the next wealthiest cities in India were [Delhi](/wiki/Delhi "Delhi") ($450
billion), [Bengaluru](/wiki/Bengaluru "Bengaluru") ($320 billion),
[Hyderabad](/wiki/Hyderabad "Hyderabad") ($310 billion),
[Kolkata](/wiki/Kolkata "Kolkata") ($290 billion), [Chennai](/wiki/Chennai
"Chennai") ($200 billion), and [Gurugram](/wiki/Gurugram "Gurugram") ($110
billion).[376][377]

The _Global Wealth Migration Review_ _2019_ report, published by New World
Wealth, found that 5,000 HNWI's emigrated from India in 2018, or about 2% of
all HNWIs in the country. [Australia](/wiki/Australia "Australia"),
[Canada](/wiki/Canada "Canada"), and United States were among the top
destination countries.[378] The report also projected that private wealth in
India would grow by around 180% to reach $22,814 billion by 2028.[375]

### Poverty

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=50 "Edit
section: Poverty")]

Main article: [Poverty in India](/wiki/Poverty_in_India "Poverty in India")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/2012_Poverty_distribution_map_in_India_by_its_states_and_union_territories.svg/250px-2012_Poverty_distribution_map_in_India_by_its_states_and_union_territories.svg.png)](/wiki/File:2012_Poverty_distribution_map_in_India_by_its_states_and_union_territories.svg)Poverty
rate map of India by prevalence in 2012, among its states and union
territories

In May 2014, the World Bank reviewed and proposed revisions to its poverty
calculation methodology of 2005 and purchasing-power-parity basis for
measuring poverty. According to the revised methodology, the world had 872.3
million people below the new poverty line, of which 179.6 million lived in
India. With 17.5% of the total world's population, India had a 20.6% share of
the world's poorest in 2013.[379] According to a 2005–2006 survey,[380] India
had about 61 million children under the age of 5 who were chronically
malnourished. A 2011 UNICEF report stated that between 1990 and 2010, India
achieved a 45 percent reduction in mortality rates under the age of 5, and now
ranks 46th of 188 countries on this metric.[381]

Since the early 1960s, successive governments have implemented various schemes
to alleviate poverty, under central planning, that have met with partial
success.[382] In 2005, the government enacted the [Mahatma Gandhi National
Rural Employment Guarantee
Act](/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi_National_Rural_Employment_Guarantee_Act "Mahatma
Gandhi National Rural Employment Guarantee Act") (MGNREGA), guaranteeing 100
days of minimum wage employment to every rural household in all the districts
of India.[383] In 2011, it was widely criticised and beset with controversy
for corrupt officials, deficit financing as the source of funds, poor quality
of infrastructure built under the programme, and unintended destructive
effects.[384][385][386] Other studies suggest that the programme has helped
reduce rural poverty in some cases.[387][388] Yet other studies report that
India's economic growth has been the driver of sustainable employment and
poverty reduction, though a sizeable population remains in poverty.[389][390]
India lifted 271 million people out of poverty between 2006 and 2016,
recording the fastest reductions in the multidimensional poverty index values
during the period with strong improvements in areas such as assets, cooking
fuel, sanitation, and nutrition.[391]

On the 2019 [Global Hunger Index](/wiki/Global_Hunger_Index "Global Hunger
Index") India [ranked 102nd](/wiki/Global_Hunger_Index#Country_rankings
"Global Hunger Index") (out of 117 countries) with a score of 30.3, being
categorized as 'serious' in severity.[392] In 2024, India ranked 105 (out of
127 countries) with a score of 27.3, indicating an improvement in its hunger
situation.[393][394]

According to the [World Bank](/wiki/World_Bank "World Bank")’s Spring [2025
Poverty and Equity
Brief](https://documents1.worldbank.org/curated/en/099722104222534584/pdf/IDU-25f34333-d3a3-44ae-8268-86830e3bc5a5.pdf),
approximately 171 million people in India moved out of extreme poverty between
2011 and 2023. During this period, the country's poverty rate declined
significantly from 16.2% to 2.3%, based on the international poverty line of
$2.15 per day.[395]

2021  | 74.98 | 101.56  
2022  | 81.35 | 96.23  
2023  | 81.94 | 100.95  
  
The [Indian rupee](/wiki/Indian_rupee "Indian rupee") (₹) is the only [legal
tender](/wiki/Legal_tender "Legal tender") in India, and is also accepted as
legal tender in neighbouring Nepal and Bhutan, both of which peg their
currency to that of the Indian rupee. The rupee previously was divided into
100 paise, which no longer exist. The highest-denomination banknote was the
₹2,000 note until 30 September 2023 after which it was scrapped and ₹500 note
became the highest denomination; the lowest-denomination coin in circulation
is the ₹1 coin.[399] In 2017, demonetisation was announced in which ₹500 and
₹1000 notes were withdrawn and new ₹500 notes were issued. India's monetary
system is managed by the Reserve Bank of India (RBI), the country's [central
bank](/wiki/Central_bank "Central bank").[400] Established on 1 April 1935 and
nationalised in 1949, the RBI serves as the nation's monetary authority,
regulator and supervisor of the monetary system, banker to the government,
custodian of foreign exchange reserves, and as an issuer of currency. It is
governed by a central board of directors, headed by a governor who is
appointed by the Government of India.[401] The benchmark [interest
rates](/wiki/Interest_rates "Interest rates") are set by the [Monetary Policy
Committee](/wiki/Monetary_Policy_Committee_of_India "Monetary Policy Committee
of India").

The rupee was linked to the [British pound](/wiki/British_pound "British
pound") from 1927 to 1946, and then to US dollar until 1975 through a [fixed
exchange rate](/wiki/Fixed_exchange_rate "Fixed exchange rate"). It was
devalued in September 1975 and the system of fixed par rate was replaced with
a basket of four major international currencies: the British pound, US dollar,
the [Japanese yen](/wiki/Japanese_yen "Japanese yen") and the [Deutsche
Mark](/wiki/Deutsche_Mark "Deutsche Mark").[402] In 1991, after the collapse
of its largest trading partner, the Soviet Union, India faced the major
foreign exchange crisis and the rupee was devalued by around 19% in two stages
on 1 and 2 July. In 1992, a Liberalized Exchange Rate Mechanism (LERMS) was
introduced. Under LERMS, exporters had to surrender 40 percent of their
foreign exchange earnings to the RBI at the RBI-determined exchange rate; the
remaining 60% could be converted at the market-determined exchange rate. In
1994, the rupee was convertible on the current account, with some capital
controls.[403]

After the sharp devaluation in 1991 and transition to current account
convertibility in 1994, the value of the rupee has been largely determined by
market forces. The rupee has been fairly stable during the decade 2000–2010.
In October 2022, rupee touched an all-time low 83.29 to US dollar.[404][405]

## Security markets

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=52 "Edit
section: Security markets")]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/IT7A2275_copy_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-
IT7A2275_copy_%28cropped%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:IT7A2275_copy_\(cropped\).jpg)The
[National Stock Exchange of India](/wiki/National_Stock_Exchange_of_India
"National Stock Exchange of India") (NSE) is the biggest [stock
exchange](/wiki/Stock_exchange "Stock exchange") in India by trading volume as
96% trading occurs in NSE

The development of Indian security markets began with the launch of the
[Bombay Stock Exchange](/wiki/Bombay_Stock_Exchange "Bombay Stock Exchange")
(BSE) in July 1875 and the [Ahmedabad Stock
Exchange](/wiki/Ahmedabad_Stock_Exchange "Ahmedabad Stock Exchange") in 1894.
Since then, 22 other exchanges have traded in Indian cities. In 2014, India's
stock exchange market became the 10th largest in the world by market
capitalisation, just above those of South Korea and Australia.[406] India's
two major stock exchanges, BSE and the [National Stock Exchange of
India](/wiki/National_Stock_Exchange_of_India "National Stock Exchange of
India"), had a market capitalisation of US$1.71 trillion and US$1.68 trillion
as of February
2015,[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit)
according to the [World Federation of
Exchanges](/wiki/World_Federation_of_Exchanges "World Federation of
Exchanges"), which grew to $3.36 trillion and $3.31 trillion respectively by
September 2021.[407][408]

The initial public offering (IPO) market in India has been small compared to
NYSE and NASDAQ, raising US$300 million in 2013 and US$1.4 billion in 2012.
[Ernst & Young](/wiki/Ernst_%26_Young "Ernst & Young") stated[409] that the
low IPO activity reflects market conditions, slow government approval
processes, and complex regulations. Before 2013, Indian companies were not
allowed to list their securities internationally without first completing an
IPO in India. In 2013, these security laws were reformed and Indian companies
can now choose where they want to list first: overseas, domestically, or both
concurrently.[410] Further, security laws have been revised to ease overseas
listings of already-listed companies, to increase liquidity for private equity
and international investors in Indian companies.[409]
