2000  | 103.9  | 43.1  | 60.8  | -17.7   
1999  | 86.5  | 36.3  | 50.2  | -13.9   
  
Until the liberalisation of 1991, India was largely and intentionally isolated
from world markets, to protect its economy and to achieve self-reliance.
Foreign trade was subject to import tariffs, export taxes and quantitative
restrictions, while [foreign direct
investment](/wiki/Foreign_direct_investment "Foreign direct investment") (FDI)
was restricted by upper-limit equity participation, restrictions on technology
transfer, export obligations and government approvals; these approvals were
needed for nearly 60% of new FDI in the industrial sector. The restrictions
ensured that FDI averaged only around $200 million annually between 1985 and
1991; a large percentage of the capital flows consisted of foreign aid,
commercial borrowing and deposits of [non-resident Indians](/wiki/Non-
resident_Indian_and_person_of_Indian_origin "Non-resident Indian and person of
Indian origin").[414] India's exports were stagnant for the first 15 years
after independence, due to general neglect of trade policy by the government
of that period; imports in the same period, with early industrialisation,
consisted predominantly of machinery, raw materials and consumer goods.[415]
Since liberalisation, the value of India's international trade has increased
sharply,[416] with the contribution of total trade in goods and services to
the GDP rising from 16% in 1990–91 to 47% in 2009–10.[417][418] Foreign trade
accounted for 48.8% of India's GDP in 2015.[419] Globally, India accounts for
1.44% of exports and 2.12% of imports for merchandise trade and 3.34% of
exports and 3.31% of imports for commercial services trade.[418] India's major
trading partners are the European Union, China, United States and [United Arab
Emirates](/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates "United Arab Emirates").[420] In 2006–07,
major export commodities included engineering goods, petroleum products,
chemicals and pharmaceuticals, gems and jewellery, textiles and garments,
agricultural products, iron ore and other minerals. Major import commodities
included crude oil and related products, machinery, electronic goods, gold and
silver.[421] In November 2010, exports increased 22.3% year-on-year to ₹851
billion (equivalent to ₹1.9 trillion or US$23 billion in 2023), while imports
were up 7.5% at ₹1.25 trillion (equivalent to ₹2.8 trillion or US$33 billion
in 2023). The trade deficit for the same month dropped from ₹469 billion
(equivalent to ₹1.2 trillion or US$14 billion in 2023) in 2009 to ₹401 billion
(equivalent to ₹900 billion or US$11 billion in 2023) in 2010.[422]

India is a founding-member of [General Agreement on Tariffs and
Trade](/wiki/General_Agreement_on_Tariffs_and_Trade "General Agreement on
Tariffs and Trade") (GATT) and its successor, the WTO. While participating
actively in its general council meetings, India has been crucial in voicing
the concerns of the [developing world](/wiki/Developing_country "Developing
country"). For instance, India has continued its opposition to the inclusion
of labour, environmental issues and other [non-tariff barriers to
trade](/wiki/Non-tariff_barriers_to_trade "Non-tariff barriers to trade") in
WTO policies.[423]

[India](/wiki/India "India") secured 43rd place in competitiveness index.[424]

### Balance of payments

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=57 "Edit
section: Balance of payments")]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Cumulative_Current_Account_Balance.png/250px-
Cumulative_Current_Account_Balance.png)](/wiki/File:Cumulative_Current_Account_Balance.png)[Cumulative](/wiki/Cumulativity
"Cumulativity") current account balance 1980–2008 based on IMF data

Since independence, India's [balance of payments](/wiki/Balance_of_payments
"Balance of payments") on its [current
account](/wiki/Current_account_\(balance_of_payments\) "Current account
\(balance of payments\)") has been negative. Since economic liberalisation in
the 1990s, precipitated by a balance-of-payment crisis, India's exports rose
consistently, covering 80.3% of its imports in 2002–03, up from 66.2% in
1990–91.[425] However, the global economic slump followed by a general
deceleration in world trade saw the exports as a percentage of imports drop to
61.4% in 2008–09.[426] India's growing oil import bill is seen as the main
driver behind the large current account deficit,[427] which rose to $118.7
billion, or 11.11% of GDP, in 2008–09.[428] Between January and October 2010,
India imported $82.1 billion worth of crude oil.[427] The Indian economy has
run a trade deficit every year from 2002 to 2012, with a merchandise trade
deficit of US$189 billion in 2011–12.[429] Its trade with China has the
largest deficit, about $31 billion in 2013.[430]

India's reliance on external assistance and concessional debt has decreased
since liberalisation of the economy, and the [debt service
ratio](/wiki/Debt_service_ratio "Debt service ratio") decreased from 35.3% in
1990–91 to 4.4% in 2008–09.[431] In India, [external commercial
borrowings](/wiki/External_commercial_borrowing_\(India\) "External commercial
borrowing \(India\)") (ECBs), or commercial loans from non-resident lenders,
are being permitted by the government for providing an additional source of
funds to Indian corporates. The [Ministry of
Finance](/wiki/Finance_Minister_of_India "Finance Minister of India") monitors
and regulates them through ECB policy guidelines issued by the Reserve Bank of
India (RBI) under the [Foreign Exchange Management
Act](/wiki/Foreign_Exchange_Management_Act "Foreign Exchange Management Act")
of 1999.[432] India's [foreign exchange
reserves](/wiki/Foreign_exchange_reserve "Foreign exchange reserve") have
steadily risen from $5.8 billion in March 1991 to ₹38,832.21 billion (US$540
billion) in July 2020.[433][434] In 2012, United Kingdom announced an end to
all financial aid to India, citing the growth and robustness of Indian
economy.[435][436]

India's current account deficit reached an all-time high in 2013.[437] India
has historically funded its current account deficit through borrowings by
companies in the overseas markets or remittances by non-resident Indians and
portfolio inflows. From April 2016 to January 2017, RBI data showed that, for
the first time since 1991, India was funding its deficit through foreign
direct investment inflows. _[The Economic Times](/wiki/The_Economic_Times "The
Economic Times")_ noted that the development was "a sign of rising confidence
among long-term investors in Prime Minister [Narendra
Modi](/wiki/Narendra_Modi "Narendra Modi")'s ability to strengthen the
country's economic foundation for sustained growth".[438]

### Foreign direct investment

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=58 "Edit
section: Foreign direct investment")]

![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/60px-
Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png)| This section needs to be **updated**.
Please help update this article to reflect recent events or newly available
information. _( October 2015)_  
---|---  
  
Main article: [Foreign direct investment in
India](/wiki/Foreign_direct_investment_in_India "Foreign direct investment in
India")

**Share of top five investing countries in FDI inflows (2000–2010)**[439] Rank | Country | Inflows  
(million US$) | Inflows (%)   
---|---|---|---  
1 | Mauritius | 50,164 | 42.00   
2 | Singapore | 11,275 | 9.00   
3 | US | 8,914 | 7.00   
4 | UK | 6,158 | 5.00   
5 | Netherlands | 4,968 | 4.00   
  
As the third-largest economy in the world in PPP terms, India has attracted
[foreign direct investment](/wiki/Foreign_direct_investment "Foreign direct
investment") (FDI).[440] During the year 2011, FDI inflow into India stood at
$36.5 billion, 51.1% higher than the 2010 figure of $24.15 billion. India has
strengths in telecommunication, information technology and other significant
areas such as auto components, chemicals, apparels, pharmaceuticals, and
jewellery. Despite a surge in foreign investments, rigid FDI policies[441]
were a significant hindrance. Over time, India has adopted a number of FDI
reforms.[440] India has a large pool of skilled managerial and technical
expertise. The size of the [middle-class](/wiki/Standard_of_living_in_India
"Standard of living in India") population stands at 300 million and represents
a growing consumer market.[442]

India liberalised its FDI policy in 2005, allowing up to a 100% FDI stake in
ventures. Industrial policy reforms have substantially reduced industrial
licensing requirements, removed restrictions on expansion and facilitated easy
access to foreign technology and investment. The upward growth curve of the
real-estate sector owes some credit to a booming economy and liberalised FDI
regime. In March 2005, the government amended the rules to allow 100% FDI in
the construction sector, including built-up infrastructure and construction
development projects comprising housing, commercial premises, hospitals,
educational institutions, recreational facilities, and city- and regional-
level infrastructure.[443] Between 2012 and 2014, India extended these reforms
to defence, telecom, oil, retail, aviation, and other sectors.[444][445]

From 2000 to 2010, the country attracted $178 billion as FDI.[446] The
inordinately high investment from [Mauritius](/wiki/Mauritius "Mauritius") is
due to routing of international funds through the country given significant
tax advantages – double taxation is avoided due to a [tax
treaty](/wiki/Double_taxation#Double_taxation_avoidance_agreement_signed_by_India
"Double taxation") between India and Mauritius, and Mauritius is a capital
gains [tax haven](/wiki/Tax_haven "Tax haven"), effectively creating a zero-
taxation FDI channel.[447] FDI accounted for 2.1% of India's GDP in 2015.[419]

As the government has eased 87 foreign investment direct rules across 21
sectors in the last three years, FDI inflows hit $60.1 billion between 2016
and 2017 in India.[448][449]

#### Outflows

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=59 "Edit
section: Outflows")]

Since 2000, Indian companies have expanded overseas, investing FDI and
creating jobs outside India. From 2006 to 2010, FDI by Indian companies
outside India amounted to 1.34 per cent of its GDP.[450] Indian companies have
deployed FDI and started operations in United States,[451] Europe and
Africa.[452] The Indian conglomerate [Tata Group](/wiki/Tata_Group "Tata
Group") is United Kingdom's largest manufacturer and private-sector
employer.[453][454]

### Remittances

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=60 "Edit
section: Remittances")]

Main article: [Remittances to India](/wiki/Remittances_to_India "Remittances
to India")

In 2015, a total of US$68.91 billion was made in remittances to India from
other countries, and a total of US$8.476 billion was made in remittances by
foreign workers in India to their home countries.
[UAE](/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates "United Arab Emirates"), US, and [Saudi
Arabia](/wiki/Saudi_Arabia "Saudi Arabia") were the top sources of remittances
to India, while Bangladesh, Pakistan, and Nepal were the top recipients of
remittances from India.[455] Remittances to India accounted for 3.32% of the
country's GDP in 2015.[419]

### Mergers and acquisitions

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=61 "Edit
section: Mergers and acquisitions")]

Between 1985 and 2018 20,846 deals have been announced in, into (inbound) and
out of (outbound) India. This cumulates to a value of US$618 billion. In terms
of value, 2010 has been the most active year with deals worth almost 60 bil.
USD. Most deals have been conducted in 2007 (1,510).[456]

## Economic issues

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=62 "Edit
section: Economic issues")]

### Corruption

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=63 "Edit
section: Corruption")]

Main articles: [Corruption in India](/wiki/Corruption_in_India "Corruption in
India") and [Indian black money](/wiki/Indian_black_money "Indian black
money")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Countries_by_Corruption_Perceptions_Index_%282023%29.svg/500px-
Countries_by_Corruption_Perceptions_Index_%282023%29.svg.png)](/wiki/File:Countries_by_Corruption_Perceptions_Index_\(2023\).svg)A
map depicting [Corruption Perceptions
Index](/wiki/Corruption_Perceptions_Index "Corruption Perceptions Index") in
other countries as compared to India in 2023; a higher score indicates lower
levels of corruption

  100 – 90

  89 – 80

  79 – 70

  69 – 60

  59 – 50

  49 – 40

  39 – 30

  29 – 20

  19 – 10

  9 – 0

  No data

Corruption has been a pervasive problem in India.[457] A 2005 study by
[Transparency International](/wiki/Transparency_International "Transparency
International") (TI) found that more than half of those surveyed had first-
hand experience of paying a bribe or peddling influence to get a job done in a
public office in the previous year.[458] A follow-up study in 2008 found this
rate to be 40 percent.[459] In 2011, TI ranked India at 95th place amongst 183
countries in perceived levels of public sector corruption.[460] By 2016, India
saw a reduction in corruption, and its ranking improved to 79th place.[461]

In 1996, [red tape](/wiki/Red_tape "Red tape"), bureaucracy, and the [Licence
Raj](/wiki/Licence_Raj "Licence Raj") were suggested as a cause for
institutionalised corruption and inefficiency.[462] More recent
reports[463][464][465] suggest the causes of corruption include excessive
regulations and approval requirements, mandated spending programs, monopoly of
certain goods and service providers by government-controlled institutions,
bureaucracy with discretionary powers, and lack of transparent laws and
processes.

Computerisation of services, various central and state vigilance commissions,
and the 2005 [Right to Information Act](/wiki/Right_to_Information_Act "Right
to Information Act") – which requires government officials to furnish
information requested by citizens or face punitive action – have considerably
reduced corruption and opened avenues to redress grievances.[458]

In 2011, the Indian government concluded that most spending fails to reach its
intended recipients, as the large and inefficient bureaucracy consumes
budgets.[466] India's absence rates are among the worst in the world; one
study found that 25% of public sector teachers and 40% of government-owned
public-sector medical workers could not be found at the workplace.[467][468]
Similarly, many issues are facing Indian scientists, with demands for
transparency, a [meritocratic](/wiki/Meritocratic "Meritocratic") system, and
an overhaul of the bureaucratic agencies that oversee science and
technology.[469]

India has an [underground economy](/wiki/Underground_economy "Underground
economy"), with a 2006 report alleging that India topped the worldwide list
for [black money](/wiki/Indian_black_money "Indian black money") with almost
$1,456 billion stashed in Swiss banks. This would amount to 13 times the
country's total external debt.[470][471] These allegations have been denied by
the [Swiss Banking Association](/wiki/Swiss_Banking_Association "Swiss Banking
Association"). James Nason, the Head of International Communications for the
Swiss Banking Association, suggested "The (black money) figures were rapidly
picked up in the Indian media and in Indian opposition circles, and circulated
as gospel truth. However, this story was a complete fabrication. The Swiss
Bankers Association never published such a report. Anyone claiming to have
such figures (for India) should be forced to identify their source and explain
the methodology used to produce them."[472][473] A Step was taken by Prime
Minister Modi, on 8 November 2016, involved the demonetization of all 500 and
1000 rupee bank notes (replaced by new 500 and 2000 rupee notes) to return
black money into the economy followed by criticism that the measure was deemed
ineffective by economists and negatively affected the poorest people of India.
This [demonetisation](/wiki/2016_Indian_banknote_demonetisation "2016 Indian
banknote demonetisation") together with the introduction of The [goods and
services tax(GST)](/wiki/Goods_and_Services_Tax_\(India\) "Goods and Services
Tax \(India\)") is believed to be responsible for the slowdown in growth.[474]

### Education

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=64 "Edit
section: Education")]

Main articles: [Education in India](/wiki/Education_in_India "Education in
India") and [Literacy in India](/wiki/Literacy_in_India "Literacy in India")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Calcutta_university_1870.jpg/250px-
Calcutta_university_1870.jpg)](/wiki/File:Calcutta_university_1870.jpg)[University
of Calcutta](/wiki/University_of_Calcutta "University of Calcutta"),
established in 1857, was the first multidisciplinary and secular Western-style
institution in Asia.

India has made progress in increasing the primary education attendance rate
and expanding [literacy](/wiki/Literacy_in_India "Literacy in India") to
approximately three-fourths of the population.[475] India's literacy rate had
grown from 52.2% in 1991 to 74.04% in 2011. The right to education at the
elementary level has been made one of the fundamental rights under the Eighty-
Sixth Amendment of 2002, and legislation has been enacted to further the
objective of providing free education to all children.[476] However, the
literacy rate of 74% is lower than the worldwide average, and the country
suffers from a high drop-out rate.[477] Literacy rates and educational
opportunities vary by region, gender, urban and rural areas, and among
different social groups.[478][479]

### Economic disparities

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=65 "Edit
section: Economic disparities")]

Main articles: [Economic disparities in
India](/wiki/Economic_disparities_in_India "Economic disparities in India")
and [Poverty in India](/wiki/Poverty_in_India "Poverty in India")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/NSDP_Per_Capita_of_Indian_States_and_UT%27s%2C_2018-19.png/250px-
NSDP_Per_Capita_of_Indian_States_and_UT%27s%2C_2018-19.png)](/wiki/File:NSDP_Per_Capita_of_Indian_States_and_UT%27s,_2018-19.png)Economic
disparities among the states and union territories of India, on GDP per
capita, [PPP basis](/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity "Purchasing power parity"),
and GDP per capita basis in 2011

> Poverty rates in India's poorest states are three to four times higher than
> those in the more advanced states. While India's average annual per capita
> income was $1,410 in 2011 – placing it among the poorest of the world's
> middle-income countries – it was just $436 in Uttar Pradesh (which has more
> people than Brazil) and only $294 in Bihar, one of India's poorest states.

— World Bank: India Country Overview 2013[480]

A critical problem facing India's economy is the sharp and growing regional
variations among India's different states and territories in terms of poverty,
availability of infrastructure, and socio-economic development.[481] Six low-
income states – [Assam](/wiki/Assam "Assam"), Chhattisgarh,
[Nagaland](/wiki/Nagaland "Nagaland"), Madhya Pradesh, [Odisha](/wiki/Odisha
"Odisha"), and [Uttar Pradesh](/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh "Uttar Pradesh") – are home
to more than one-third of India's population.[482] Severe disparities exist
among states in terms of income, literacy rates, life expectancy, and living
conditions.[483] The four states of Maharashtra, Tamil Nadu, Gujarat and
Karnataka alone are projected to account for almost 50% of India's GDP by
2030; the five [South Indian states](/wiki/South_India "South India") are
projected to contribute 35% of India's GDP by 2030 despite currently having
20% of India's population.[484]

The five-year plans, especially in the pre-liberalisation era, attempted to
reduce regional disparities by encouraging industrial development in the
interior regions and distributing industries across states. The results have
been discouraging as these measures increased inefficiency and hampered
effective industrial growth.[485] The more advanced states have been better
placed to benefit from liberalisation, with well-developed infrastructure and
an educated and skilled workforce, which attract the manufacturing and service
sectors. Governments of less-advanced states have tried to reduce disparities
by offering tax holidays and cheap land and focused on sectors like tourism,
which can develop faster than other sectors.[486][487] India's income [Gini
coefficient](/wiki/Gini_coefficient "Gini coefficient") is 33.9, according to
the United Nations Development Programme (UNDP), indicating overall income
distribution to be more uniform than East Asia, Latin America, and Africa.[14]
The _Global Wealth Migration Review_ _2019_ report, published by New World
Wealth, estimated that 48% of India's total wealth was held by high-net-worth
individuals.[375] According to a 2021 report by the [Pew Research
Center](/wiki/Pew_Research_Center "Pew Research Center"), India has roughly
1.2 billion lower-income individuals, 66 million middle-income individuals, 16
million upper-middle-income individuals, and 2 million in the high-income
group.[488] As per _[The Economist](/wiki/The_Economist "The Economist")_ , 78
million of India's population are considered middle class as of 2017, if
defined using the cutoff of those making more than $10 per day, a standard
used by the India's [National Council of Applied Economic
Research](/wiki/National_Council_of_Applied_Economic_Research "National
Council of Applied Economic Research").[489]

There is a continuing debate on whether India's economic expansion has been
pro-poor or anti-poor.[490] Studies suggest that urban economic growth has
become less pro-poor since the [1991 economic
reforms](/wiki/Economic_liberalisation_in_India "Economic liberalisation in
India").[490][491]

### Climate change

[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Economy_of_India&action=edit&section=66 "Edit
section: Climate change")]

Several banks including State Bank of India, Axis Bank, ICICI Bank and HDFC
Bank have taken the lead in funding green projects even as the Reserve Bank of
India comes up with norms for lenders to disclose their actions on climate
risk. RBI in an earlier report in 2022, had said that climate change would
require "intensive capital mobilisation" for that India needs $17.77
trillion.[492]

The RBI recommends that regulated entities rely upon the Task Force on
Climate-related Financial Disclosures (TCFD) framework, the most prominent
climate disclosure framework globally, at least at the initial stage.[493]

India is home to 75 per cent of the world's wild tiger population. The big
cats like lion, tiger, leopard, cheetah, snow leopard, jaguar and puma were on
the brink of extinction. With hunting and encroachment, their numbers dropped
to a mere 20. Efforts to save the lion started before Independence but it was
in 1965 that the Indian Forest Service stepped in to set up a conservation
programme. Researchers believe a combination of factors is driving the
unprecedented migration in tigers. Rising temperatures due to climate change
are making the lower foothills less hospitable.[494]
